      <section class="article-section__content" id="batt202300409-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="batt202300409-sec-0010-title"> Results and discussion</h2>
         
         <p>In this section, the main results of stochastic 3D modeling of nanostructured NVP/C-particles are discussed. First, we validate the proposed stochastic 3D struture model in Section 4.2. Second, in Section 4.2., the validated model is used for a simulation-based prediction on how the amount of carbon influences the morphology and thus effective properties of the nanostructured active material particles.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="batt202300409-sec-0011">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="batt202300409-sec-0011-title"> Model validation</h3>
            
            <p>For validating the stochastic structure model, we simulate three realizations of the calibrated model with the parameters shown in Table <a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#batt202300409-tbl-0001">1</a> with a cubic voxel size of 16.6 nm<sup>3</sup>. The nanostructures are realized within cubes of side length <img alt="urn:x-wiley:25666223:media:batt202300409:batt202300409-math-0127" loading="lazy" class="section_image" src="/cms/asset/331c447c-bf36-4688-913c-53f2ca99eb20/batt202300409-math-0127.png">
. Figure <a href="#batt202300409-fig-0002">2</a> shows a comparison between a segmented HR-SEM image and a 2D cross-section of a model realization. Here one can observe a good visual fit, which is quantitatively underpinned by comparing morphological descriptors.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="batt202300409-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/7a709c1e-2a89-4a0c-b831-be3ba995bdc3/batt202300409-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/7a709c1e-2a89-4a0c-b831-be3ba995bdc3/batt202300409-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/acd4879c-467b-4b33-a389-aa5f6d5db387/batt202300409-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/7a709c1e-2a89-4a0c-b831-be3ba995bdc3/batt202300409-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=batt202300409-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fbatt.202300409" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>Comparison between greyscale image obtained by 2D SEM (a), the corresponding segmented image data (b) and a virtual nanostructure generated by the stochastic 3D model (c). NVP, carbon, and pores are represented in yellow, red and blue, respectively.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>For the quantitative validation, we consider the two-point coverage probability functions introduced in Section 3.2. as well as the 2D continuous phase size distributions.<span><sup><a href="#batt202300409-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202300409-bib-0030_R_d118525043e1343" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - J. Mater. Sci.">30</a></sup></span> The two-point coverage probability functions are shown in Figure <a href="#batt202300409-fig-0003">3</a>. The functions are centered here, i. e., we show the functions <img alt="urn:x-wiley:25666223:media:batt202300409:batt202300409-math-0128" loading="lazy" class="section_image" src="/cms/asset/ed49dde5-7c21-4b22-9133-b93ddcdbfc64/batt202300409-math-0128.png">
, such that all functions asymptotically tend to 0. It is evident that we have a nearly perfect fit of the model with respect to these descriptors. Even if these functions have been used for model calibration, the good accordance can be considered as model validation regarding the following two aspects. First, Figure <a href="#batt202300409-fig-0003">3</a> (a) shows the consistency between FIB-SEM images with a voxel size of <img alt="urn:x-wiley:25666223:media:batt202300409:batt202300409-math-0129" loading="lazy" class="section_image" src="/cms/asset/742a9eef-3ed7-4530-a731-86493cb391a7/batt202300409-math-0129.png">
 and the SEM images with a pixel size of 5 nm. Recall from Section 3 that the correlation function <i>ρ<sub>X</sub></i>, which determines <i>C</i><sub>11</sub> is estimated based on FIB-SEM data, while the Figure <a href="#batt202300409-fig-0003">3</a> (a) compares <i>C</i><sub>11</sub> computed from model realizations with <i>C</i><sub>11</sub> estimated from the three HR-SEM images which are used to quantify the spatial distribution of carbon and pores. Second, the results shown in Figure <a href="#batt202300409-fig-0003">3</a> justify the particular choice of the covariance functions <i>ρ<sub>X</sub></i> and <i>ρ<sub>Y</sub></i> given in Equations (8) and (12), which both depend only on one model parameter. It is not clear <i>a priori</i> that this choice allows to fit all two-point coverage probability functions as well as it is shown in Figure <a href="#batt202300409-fig-0003">3</a>.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="batt202300409-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c6d7c625-20b9-4c0b-96bb-cf9c8eb16b44/batt202300409-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/c6d7c625-20b9-4c0b-96bb-cf9c8eb16b44/batt202300409-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/5940add3-3a01-4ac9-9a39-045ef45dd073/batt202300409-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c6d7c625-20b9-4c0b-96bb-cf9c8eb16b44/batt202300409-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=batt202300409-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fbatt.202300409" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>Centered two-point coverage probability functions <i>C<sub>ij</sub> ϵ<sub>i</sub>ϵ<sub>j</sub></i> estimated from HR-SEM images and simulated data. The cases <i>i</i>=<i>j</i>=1 (a), <i>i</i>=<i>j</i>=2 (b), <i>i</i>=<i>j</i>=3 (c), <i>i</i>=1 and <i>j</i>=2 (d), <i>i</i>=1 and <i>j</i>=3 (e) as well as <i>i</i>=2 and <i>j</i>=3 (f) are shown. Recall that represent NVP, carbon, and pores, respectively.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">Moreover, the 2D continuous phase size distribution of model realizations and HR-SEM images are compared in Figure <a href="#batt202300409-fig-0004">4</a>. The 2D continuous phase size distribution <img alt="urn:x-wiley:25666223:media:batt202300409:batt202300409-math-0130" loading="lazy" class="section_image" src="/cms/asset/fa22e63a-30a6-48b7-848d-b819bf39d86d/batt202300409-math-0130.png">
 of the phase Ξ<sub><i>i</i></sub>, <img alt="urn:x-wiley:25666223:media:batt202300409:batt202300409-math-0131" loading="lazy" class="section_image" src="/cms/asset/3fffa45a-d62b-4190-8881-17222927f8f5/batt202300409-math-0131.png">
 is defined by
               <div class="inline-equation" id="batt202300409-disp-0013"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><img class="" src="/cms/asset/93abd885-578f-46bb-b1a2-71fa538c0c2f/batt202300409-math-0132.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:25666223:media:batt202300409:batt202300409-math-0132" title="urn:x-wiley:25666223:media:batt202300409:batt202300409-math-0132" loading="lazy"></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(13)</span></div>
               </div>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="batt202300409-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/b40f58ec-9aca-4c8c-af3f-ab3d1235279b/batt202300409-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/b40f58ec-9aca-4c8c-af3f-ab3d1235279b/batt202300409-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/50ffe3f0-491f-4ea5-a2c9-19fdb4684853/batt202300409-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/b40f58ec-9aca-4c8c-af3f-ab3d1235279b/batt202300409-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=batt202300409-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fbatt.202300409" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>Comparison of 2D continuous phase size distributions between HR-SEM images and simulated data. The green curves represent the continuous phase size distributions computed for each slice of the simulated 3D data. The blue dotted lines show the continuous phase size distributions of the three HR-SEM images, the mean of which is represented in red. Results are shown for NVP (a), carbon (b) and pores (c).</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>where <img alt="urn:x-wiley:25666223:media:batt202300409:batt202300409-math-0133" loading="lazy" class="section_image" src="/cms/asset/89c23def-07ab-4ed7-a945-63c6b266d82d/batt202300409-math-0133.png">
 with two-dimensional Lebesgue measure <img alt="urn:x-wiley:25666223:media:batt202300409:batt202300409-math-0134" loading="lazy" class="section_image" src="/cms/asset/a82eaa3c-e908-43d8-b831-ee7bf2843c4f/batt202300409-math-0134.png">
 and <img alt="urn:x-wiley:25666223:media:batt202300409:batt202300409-math-0135" loading="lazy" class="section_image" src="/cms/asset/04a27533-e406-430c-90d0-677f1dc52d5f/batt202300409-math-0135.png">
 denotes the disk centered at the origin <img alt="urn:x-wiley:25666223:media:batt202300409:batt202300409-math-0136" loading="lazy" class="section_image" src="/cms/asset/9c4e3637-5e84-4b3f-9ceb-aac6b7ab244b/batt202300409-math-0136.png">
 with radius <img alt="urn:x-wiley:25666223:media:batt202300409:batt202300409-math-0137" loading="lazy" class="section_image" src="/cms/asset/b40fc8c8-d1e6-404a-ac27-820a177cb946/batt202300409-math-0137.png">
 In other words, the continuous phase size distribution of Ξ<sub><i>i</i></sub> at <img alt="urn:x-wiley:25666223:media:batt202300409:batt202300409-math-0138" loading="lazy" class="section_image" src="/cms/asset/3d2f4f5a-dc90-4ce2-8376-9dac40974c9c/batt202300409-math-0138.png">
 is the volume fraction of the morphological opening<span><sup><a href="#batt202300409-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202300409-bib-0031_R_d118525043e1509" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31">31</a></sup></span> of Ξ<sub><i>i</i></sub> with a disk of radius <img alt="urn:x-wiley:25666223:media:batt202300409:batt202300409-math-0139" loading="lazy" class="section_image" src="/cms/asset/102d82e4-952e-44f6-b08c-907322bc4fe0/batt202300409-math-0139.png">
 as structuring element, which is normalized by the volume fraction of Ξ<sub><i>i</i></sub> itself. Note that this opening can be considered as the subset of Ξ<sub><i>i</i></sub> which can be covered by a union of disks <img alt="urn:x-wiley:25666223:media:batt202300409:batt202300409-math-0140" loading="lazy" class="section_image" src="/cms/asset/e285c7c4-4f0c-489a-b902-a8300e11de80/batt202300409-math-0140.png">
 where each disk is completely contained in Ξ<sub><i>i</i></sub>. The continuous phase size distribution carries the same information as the granulometry function in mathematical morphology,<span><sup><a href="#batt202300409-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202300409-bib-0015_R_d118525043e1529" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15">15</a>, <a href="#batt202300409-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202300409-bib-0032_R_d118525043e1529" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32">32</a></sup></span> which is a widely used descriptor measuring the size distribution of complex structures. We estimate the continuous phase distribution at <img alt="urn:x-wiley:25666223:media:batt202300409:batt202300409-math-0141" loading="lazy" class="section_image" src="/cms/asset/c467ffe1-dd74-40d3-809f-7a46e601497c/batt202300409-math-0141.png">
 from image data by estimating the volume fraction of the morphological opening of Ξ<sub><i>i</i></sub> with structuring element <img alt="urn:x-wiley:25666223:media:batt202300409:batt202300409-math-0142" loading="lazy" class="section_image" src="/cms/asset/79df189d-e372-4642-921d-740f373d6ba8/batt202300409-math-0142.png">
 Edge effects are avoided by means of minus-sampling as described in Section 4.7.2 of.<span><sup><a href="#batt202300409-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202300409-bib-0014_R_d118525043e1540" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14">14</a></sup></span> Figure <a href="#batt202300409-fig-0004">4</a> shows that the mean 2D continuous phase distribution of the HR-SEM images shows a rather similar behavior than the 2D continuous phase size distributions of the cross-sections of the simulated data. In particular for NVP, the fit is good in the sense that the mean curve computed from the HR-SEM images is completely contained within the domain in which the curves computed from the cross-sections of FIB-SEM data are located. Even if slight deviations are observed for carbon and pores, the model captures the continuous phase size distribution well. Note that these results validate the choice of the stochastic structure model described in Section 3.1. since continuous phase size distributions have not been used for model calibration. Note that a good fit of the two-point coverage distributions does not necessarily imply a good fit with respect to the continuous phase size distributions, see the discussion in Section 4.3 of.<span><sup><a href="#batt202300409-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202300409-bib-0021_R_d118525043e1546" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Comput. Mater. Sci.">21</a></sup></span></p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="batt202300409-sec-0012">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="batt202300409-sec-0012-title"> Predictive simulation: Varying the volume fraction of carbon</h3>
            
            <p>Having validated the stochastic model, it can be used for predictive simulation of virtual, but realistic NVP/C electrodes where the volume fraction of carbon is varied for a constant NVP volume fraction of 0.5148, which has been estimated from image data (Section 3.2.). Based on these virtual nanostructures, effective transport properties, i. e., effective conductivity and effective diffusivity of the three phases respectively, are predicted for different volume fractions of carbon by means of the empirical relationships derived in.<span><sup><a href="#batt202300409-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202300409-bib-0033_R_d118525043e1557" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - AIChE J.">33</a>, <a href="#batt202300409-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202300409-bib-0034_R_d118525043e1557" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Int. J. Solids Struct.">34</a></sup></span> Recall from Section 2 that the volume fraction of carbon means the volume fraction of carbon and nanopores within carbon that are not resolved in the segmentation of HR-SEM data. Under the assumption that computations in Section 2.1. correctly reflect the mere volume fraction of carbon without nanopores, we propose the following recomputation. The mere volume fraction of carbon in the complement of NVP computed in Section 2.1. is 0.28, while – due to possible nanopores that are not resolved in HR-SEM – the same fraction obtained from HR-SEM data is 0.69 (Section 3.2.). The volume fraction of carbon without nanopores can then be computed by multiplying the volume fractions of carbon with small nanopores by <img alt="urn:x-wiley:25666223:media:batt202300409:batt202300409-math-0143" loading="lazy" class="section_image" src="/cms/asset/2675d361-b177-4905-89f7-ad3e41df581b/batt202300409-math-0143.png">
               </p>
            
            <p>For this purpose, we keep all model parameters fix except of <i>λ<sub>Z</sub></i>, which controls the volume fraction of carbon denoted by <i>ϵ</i><sub>2</sub>. We consider <img alt="urn:x-wiley:25666223:media:batt202300409:batt202300409-math-0144" loading="lazy" class="section_image" src="/cms/asset/a7755a10-d2a6-433f-a4be-133af51720ac/batt202300409-math-0144.png">
. For a fixed value of <i>ϵ</i><sub>2</sub>, we plug-in <i>ϵ</i><sub>2</sub>, <i>λ<sub>X</sub></i>, <i>m</i> into Equation (9). Due to the monotonicity of the right-hand side in <i>λ</i><sub><i>Z</i>,</sub> we can numerically solve for <i>λ<sub>Z</sub></i> using the method of bisection. This means, that for each predefined value of the volume fraction of carbon <i>ϵ</i><sub>2,</sub> we obtain the full set of model parameters, which can then be used to generate virtual NVP/C nanostructures. We generate three realizations for each <img alt="urn:x-wiley:25666223:media:batt202300409:batt202300409-math-0145" loading="lazy" class="section_image" src="/cms/asset/e5a90071-7d4e-46d6-b882-234f59a0f871/batt202300409-math-0145.png">
. Corresponding 3D visualizations are represented in Figure <a href="#batt202300409-fig-0005">5</a>. It is important to note that these predictive simulations are based on the simplifying assumption that varying the amount of carbon does only influence the model parameter <i>λ</i><sub><i>Z</i>.</sub> A validation of this assumption would require further image data of NVP/C particles synthesized with different amounts of carbon. Under the same assumption, we also perform simulations to generate virtual structures with an NVP volume fraction of 0.6. This means that after changing <i>λ<sub>X</sub></i> accordingly, we generate structures with the same covariance functions of the underlying random fields. For the volume fraction of carbon, we consider <img alt="urn:x-wiley:25666223:media:batt202300409:batt202300409-math-0146" loading="lazy" class="section_image" src="/cms/asset/5cf5a38d-7984-4d45-a517-7ccaacdd2b47/batt202300409-math-0146.png">
, which ensures a porosity of at least 0.05.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="batt202300409-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/43d554c1-efcc-4290-8893-cf2551089078/batt202300409-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/43d554c1-efcc-4290-8893-cf2551089078/batt202300409-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ec34d1ab-4eb1-44f0-b15b-3f99c4e9c8be/batt202300409-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/43d554c1-efcc-4290-8893-cf2551089078/batt202300409-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=batt202300409-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fbatt.202300409" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>3D rendering of virtual NVP/C nanostructures with 0.2 (a), 0.3 (b) and 0.4 (c) volume fraction of carbon (including small nanopores). The size of each structure is <img alt="urn:x-wiley:25666223:media:batt202300409:batt202300409-math-0147" loading="lazy" class="section_image" src="/cms/asset/8efc773b-b86f-4d43-8b14-f055e01038c4/batt202300409-math-0147.png">
 NVP is represented in yellow, carbon in red and pores in blue.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>For the virtual structures, morphological descriptors, which are electrochemically important, such as the pairwise interface area between the phases, mean geodesic tortuosity quantifying the length of shortest transportation paths as well as constrictivity which is a measure for botteleneck effects are computed. In combination with the volume fractions, the latter two descriptors are used to predict the effective transport properties of the virtual structures. Recall that the volume fractions of the three phases are adjusted by the parameters of the stochastic model.</p>
            
            <p>For estimating the interface areas from image data, we exploit the algorithm presented in Ref. [35] to compute the surface area of each phase. From this information, we can compute the pairwise interface areas using Equation (3) of Ref. [36] Figure <a href="#batt202300409-fig-0006">6a</a> shows the pairwise interface areas per volume, dependent on the volume fraction of carbon. For the interface area per volume between NVP and carbon and between NVP and pores, we can observe a linear increase and decrease, respectively, for an increasing volume fraction of carbon. The relationship between the interface area of carbon and pores and the volume fraction of carbon, on the contrary, is not linear. Keeping in mind that the volume fraction of NVP is fixed in our simulations, the behavior shown in Figure <a href="#batt202300409-fig-0006">6a</a> is reasonable. The higher the carbon content is in the nanostructured particles, the less pore space is there and thus the interface area per volume between carbon and pores will have a maximum for volume fraction of carbon about 0.25. Figure <a href="#batt202300409-fig-0006">6d</a> shows the interface areas per volume for NVP volume fractions of 0.6. The qualitative behavior is the same, while the interface areas per volume between NVP and carbon are increasing and the interface areas of the other two pairs of phases are decreasing. Moreover, the maximum of the interface area per volume between carbon and pores is shifted to a carbon volume fraction of 0.2.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="batt202300409-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/fe24d650-fde7-4e50-8f6b-3f33b01617e1/batt202300409-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/fe24d650-fde7-4e50-8f6b-3f33b01617e1/batt202300409-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/19891d91-0567-4837-9ddc-6d81ef024d11/batt202300409-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/fe24d650-fde7-4e50-8f6b-3f33b01617e1/batt202300409-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=batt202300409-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fbatt.202300409" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>Influence of the volume fraction of carbon (including small nanopores) on the pairwise interface area per unit volume (a,d), mean geodesic tortuosity (b,e), and the <i>M</i>-factor (c,f) predicted by Equation (15). The results are shown for NVP volume fractions of 0.5148 (a,b,c) and 0.6000 (d,e,f). Note that the value of 0.5148 is estimated from image data. For mean geodesic tortuosity of pores and carbon, a comparison with a fitted percolation model (black dotted line) is shown.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">Mean geodesic tortuosity denoted by <img alt="urn:x-wiley:25666223:media:batt202300409:batt202300409-math-0148" loading="lazy" class="section_image" src="/cms/asset/3ee3ace0-06f9-476d-b54e-882603e000cd/batt202300409-math-0148.png">
 of a phase is defined as the quotient of the expected length of shortest paths through the considered phase, divided by the thickness of the material.<span><sup><a href="#batt202300409-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202300409-bib-0034_R_d118525043e1687" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Int. J. Solids Struct.">34</a></sup></span> Note that mean geodesic tortuosity is a purely morphological descriptor. For an overview about different notions of tortuosity, we refer to.<span><sup><a href="#batt202300409-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202300409-bib-0037_R_d118525043e1690" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Geol. Soc. Spec. Publ.">37</a>-<a href="#batt202300409-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202300409-bib-0039_R_d118525043e1690" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39">39</a></sup></span> A formal definition of mean geodesic tortuosity in the framework of random closed sets can be found in.<span><sup><a href="#batt202300409-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202300409-bib-0040_R_d118525043e1693" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Scand. Stat. Theory Appl.">40</a></sup></span> Figures <a href="#batt202300409-fig-0006">6b</a> and <a href="#batt202300409-fig-0006">6e</a> show how mean geodesic tortuosity of the three phases depends on the volume fraction of carbon. As expected, mean geodesic tortuosity of a phase correlates strongly with its volume fraction. The values for NVP are low in both cases. The mean geodesic tortuosity of the pore space, which gives information about the length of shortest pathways for ions being transported through the pores, is lower than the mean geodesic tortuosity of carbon until the volume fraction of carbon is higher than the porosity. The values of the pore space are strongly increasing with an increasing volume fraction of carbon meaning that the pore space is reduced. At some point there is the percolation threshold of the pore space, at which the mean geodesic tortuosity of the pore space tends to infinity. At these or higher volume fractions of carbon, there are no pathways through the pore space from one side to the other in the cube of side length 4.98&nbsp;μm. Additionally, we quantify the relationship between volume fraction of a phase and its geodesic tortuosity by means of regression analysis. We assume that mean geodesic tortuosity <img alt="urn:x-wiley:25666223:media:batt202300409:batt202300409-math-0149" loading="lazy" class="section_image" src="/cms/asset/4c14b5c3-3e5a-4ea3-8a84-2f39550cb805/batt202300409-math-0149.png">
 can be expressed by
               <div class="inline-equation" id="batt202300409-disp-0014"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><img class="" src="/cms/asset/143dc483-84c8-4bf4-bbfa-74225b36bc9c/batt202300409-math-0150.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:25666223:media:batt202300409:batt202300409-math-0150" title="urn:x-wiley:25666223:media:batt202300409:batt202300409-math-0150" loading="lazy"></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(14)</span></div>
               </div>
            
            <p>for each <img alt="urn:x-wiley:25666223:media:batt202300409:batt202300409-math-0151" loading="lazy" class="section_image" src="/cms/asset/06f3f187-7aab-4c00-b9e5-2877243c3a69/batt202300409-math-0151.png">
, where <i>ϵ<sub>p</sub></i> is the percolation threshold and <img alt="urn:x-wiley:25666223:media:batt202300409:batt202300409-math-0152" loading="lazy" class="section_image" src="/cms/asset/ff2dccaa-fd30-4700-a3c9-8fe8605b2acb/batt202300409-math-0152.png">
 Based on the values for <i>ϵ</i> and <img alt="urn:x-wiley:25666223:media:batt202300409:batt202300409-math-0153" loading="lazy" class="section_image" src="/cms/asset/1fecedf5-b602-4bb6-a948-40e267a96a1e/batt202300409-math-0153.png">
, the parameters <i>ϵ<sub>p</sub></i> and <i>γ</i> are estimated by nonlinear regression using the toolbox cftool in Matlab. The fitted parameters are provided in Table <a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#batt202300409-tbl-0002">2</a>. With the fitted parameters, the relationship between volume fraction and mean geodesic tortuosity can be well described, see Figures <a href="#batt202300409-fig-0006">6b</a> and <a href="#batt202300409-fig-0006">6e</a>. The percolation thresholds <i>ϵ<sub>p</sub></i> are all between 0.09 and 0.13. For pores and carbon, the scaling exponents <i>γ</i> are slightly different but are not affected when changing the NVP volume fraction from about 0.5 to 0.6. Parametric functions of the form in Equation (14) are useful to predict a wide range of morphological properties that are related to percolation phenomena, see Chapter 9 in Ref. [41]. Interestingly, even if mean geodesic tortuosity is a purely morphological descriptor, its dependence on volume fraction can be described by a power law similar to the Bruggeman relationship.
</p>

                           <td class="left-aligned"><p>0.5</p></td>
                           
                           <td class="left-aligned"><p>0.1332</p></td>
                           
                           <td class="left-aligned"><p>−0.0763</p></td>
                           </tr>
                        
                        <tr>
                           
                           <td class="right-bordered-cell left-aligned"><p>Carbon</p></td>
                           
                           <td class="left-aligned"><p>0.6</p></td>
                           
                           <td class="left-aligned"><p>0.1276</p></td>
                           
                           <td class="left-aligned"><p>−0.0775</p></td>
                           </tr>
                        </tbody>
                     </table>
               </div>
               
               
               <div class="article-section__table-source"></div>
            </div>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">An important quantity determining the effective properties of aggregated active material particles are the effective transport properties, namely effective diffusivity and effective conductivity, of the pore space, NVP and carbon, respectively. The <i>M</i>-factor, see Section 5.2 in Ref. [39], is defined as the ratio of effective and intrinsic diffusivity (and analogously conductivity). Together with constrictivity <i>β</i>, the information contained in the volume fraction and mean geodesic tortuosity can be used to predict the <i>M</i>-factor. Constrictivity <i>β</i> is a descriptor for the strength of bottlenecks effects, given as squared ratio of the width of the typical bottleneck obtained from simulated mercury intrusion porosimetry over the median of the 3D continuous phase size distribution, see Ref. [30, 40] for details. An empirically derived prediction <img alt="urn:x-wiley:25666223:media:batt202300409:batt202300409-math-0154" loading="lazy" class="section_image" src="/cms/asset/4c5b085d-480e-4a27-8522-be743e9eddb1/batt202300409-math-0154.png">
 for the <i>M</i>-factor<span><sup><a href="#batt202300409-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202300409-bib-0033_R_d118525043e1878" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - AIChE J.">33</a></sup></span> is given by
               <div class="inline-equation" id="batt202300409-disp-0015"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><img class="" src="/cms/asset/e850352b-0370-4f0c-89ef-9fac1d897a8a/batt202300409-math-0155.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:25666223:media:batt202300409:batt202300409-math-0155" title="urn:x-wiley:25666223:media:batt202300409:batt202300409-math-0155" loading="lazy"></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(15)</span></div>
               </div>
            
            <p>Note that this prediction formula has been particularly validated for nanostructured NMC particles by finite element modeling in Ref. [18]. The predictions of <i>M</i>-factors are shown in Figure <a href="#batt202300409-fig-0006">6c</a>. When increasing the NVP volume fraction from 0.5 to 0.6, the corresponding predicted <i>M</i>-factor increases from 0.3 to 0.4. For the pore space and carbon, we observe an almost linear decrease and increase, respectively, of the <i>M</i>-factor when increasing the volume fraction of carbon. The <i>M</i>-factor becomes 0, when the percolation threshold is reached. This effect can be observed for decreasing porosities and coincides with the results obtained for mean geodesic tortuosity. Moreover, as observed for mean geodesic tortuosity, the curves of <i>M</i>-factors of pores and carbon intersect with each other around the point, where the volume fractions of both phases are identical.</p>
            
            <p>The <i>M</i>-factors of nanostructured active material particles quantify the microstructure influence on effective conductivity and effective diffusivity, respectively, in the three phases. <i>M</i>-factors close to 1 mean that the microstructure has hardly a limiting effect on these effective properties, while values close to 0 indicate strong limitations.<span><sup><a href="#batt202300409-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202300409-bib-0034_R_d118525043e1913" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Int. J. Solids Struct.">34</a></sup></span> The <i>M</i>-factors on the nano-scale are crucial for the electrochemical performance of the sodium-ion cells, as they significantly impact effective transport properties on the micro-scale, i. e. effective properties of the ensemble of active material particles surrounded by a binder-additive phase. The effective properties on the micro-scale, in turn, have an important impact on the electrochemical performance of the cell. A modeling framework allowing for simulating the electrochemical performance of batteries with nanostructured cathode materials is given in<span><sup><a href="#batt202300409-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202300409-bib-0042_R_d118525043e1918" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Batteries &amp; Supercaps">42</a></sup></span> for lithium-ion batteries. In particular, the influence of nanoporosity on the specific capacity at different C-rates is discussed.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="batt202300409-sec-0013">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="batt202300409-sec-0013-title"> Conclusions</h2>
         
         <p>In the present paper, we have used stochastic 3D modeling to generate virtual structures which are statistically similar to the nanostructure of NVP/C particles used as active material for sodium-ion batteries. Model calibration has been performed based on image data. For this purpose, experimental data sets have been obtained with FIB-SEM, high-resolution SEM, EDX and TEM. While the morphology of NVP can be appropriately reconstructed from FIB-SEM data, high-resolution SEM is required to resolve the morphology of carbon and filled pores. The Pluri-Gaussian model introduced in Ref. [21], is calibrated using both, FIB-SEM and HR-SEM data. Here it is important to note that statistical techniques are used which allow to estimate these parameters of the 3D model, which concern the spatial distribution of carbon and pores, merely based on 2D SEM data. The good agreement between model and data in terms of morphological descriptors which are not used for model calibration validates the choice of the Pluri-Gaussian model. Finally, the parameters of the stochastic model are systematically varied for virtual scenario analysis. In particular, the influence of the volume fraction of carbon on electrochemically important descriptors such as the area of interfaces per unit volume and effective transport properties is quantitatively investigated. These values are important input parameters for modeling and simulation of effective properties of nanostructured NVP/C active material particles within the composite electrode, which strongly influence the performance of the battery.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="batt202300409-sec-0014">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="batt202300409-sec-0014-title"> Supplementary information</h2>
         
         <p>As supplementary information, results of EDX (Figure S1) and TEM (Figure S2) measurements are visualized.</p>
         </section>
